import torch
from transformers import AutoConfig, AutoModelForCausalLM


class Model(torch.nn.Module):
    def __init__(self, model_name, config):
        super().__init__()
        self.model_name = model_name
        self.config = config
        self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(self.model_name, config=self.config)

    def forward(self, x):
        return self.model(x).logits


model_name = "deepseek-ai/deepseek-llm-7b-base"
config = AutoConfig.from_pretrained(model_name)
vocab_size = config.vocab_size
sequence_length = 512
batch_size = 4


def get_inputs():
    inputs = torch.randint(0, vocab_size, (batch_size, sequence_length))
    return [inputs]


def get_init_inputs():
    return [model_name, config]

Actually, for the attention kernel, instead of writing my own, I could use `cublasGemmStridedBatchedEx` for QK^T and PV, then a custom kernel for softmax with causal mask. For seq=512, the attention matmuls are small but numerous (batch*heads = 128). `cublasGemmStridedBatchedEx` handles this well. Let me think about the exact math more carefully. For attention: - Q, K, V shapes after QKV projection and reshape: [batch, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim] - For DeepSeek 7B: batch=4, heads=32, seq=512, head_dim=128 - QK^T: [4, 32, 512, 128] @ [4, 32, 128, 512] → [4, 32, 512, 512] - This is 128 independent matmuls of 512×128 @ 128×512 - Using strided batched cuBLAS with batch=128 Actually, `cublasGemmStridedBatchedEx` requires the batches to be uniformly strided. Since Q, K, V are stored as [batch, seq, num_heads*head_dim] initially, after reshape they might not have uniform stride for the batch dimension. In standard PyTorch, you'd reshape to [batch*num_heads, seq, head_dim] and use batched matmul with batch=batch*num_heads=128. Then each matrix in the batch has stride seq*head_dim. So QK^T: [128, 512, 128] @ [128, 128, 512] → [128, 512, 512] PV: [128, 512, 512] @ [128, 512, 128] → [128, 512, 128] Yes, this works with strided batched GEMM. For the causal mask, I can either: 1. Compute the full QK^T, then set upper triangle to -inf, then softmax 2. Fuse causal masking into the softmax kernel Option 2 is better (saves memory bandwidth). After computing QK^T and scaling by 1/sqrt(head_dim), I run a custom kernel that applies causal mask and softmax in one pass. The softmax kernel can be implemented with online softmax for numerical stability: 1. For each row, find max value 2. Compute exp(x - max) 3. Sum the exps 4. Divide by sum 5. Set causal positions to 0 (or skip them) For numerical equivalence to PyTorch, the softmax in PyTorch uses a similar online algorithm. The key is using the same precision. PyTorch's softmax in fp32 uses fp32 accumulation. If I do the same, results should match. For the attention algorithm: 1. Load Q tile into smem 2. For each K tile: a. Load K tile, compute QK^T tile in registers b. Apply scale and causal mask c. Update online softmax stats (m, l) d. For seq=512, the number of tiles is small (8 Q tiles × 8 K tiles), so it's very efficient. Writing this kernel is definitely feasible. It would be about 200-300 lines of CUDA. In `transformers` implementation, they might compute q, k, v separately: ```python query_states = self.q_proj(hidden_states) key_states = self.k_proj(hidden_states) value_states = self.v_proj(hidden_states) ``` This means three GEMMs: [2048, 4096] @ [4096, 4096] each. Fused QKV would be one GEMM: [2048, 4096] @ [4096, 12288]. Fused is faster for two reasons: 1. Fewer kernel launches 2. Better weight memory access pattern (read 12288 columns sequentially instead of 3 separate reads of 4096 columns) Wait, actually for M=2048, reading 12288 columns vs 3×4096 columns is reading the same total amount of memory. The difference is: 1. One cuBLAS call vs three 2. Inside cuBLAS, a larger N dimension might allow better tiling For M=2048, N=12288, K=4096: - This is a reasonably large matmul - cuBLAS will achieve good utilization For three separate M=2048, N=4096, K=4096: - Each is smaller - Might have slightly lower utilization - But 3× launches overhead Fused QKV can be ~10-20% faster. So it's worth doing. But to do fused QKV, I need the weights to be physically fused. In my `ModelNew.__init__`, I can concatenate the q, k, v weights: ```python qkv_weight = torch.cat([q_proj.weight, k_proj.weight, v_proj.weight], dim=0) # or dim=1 depending on shape ``` Actually, linear weights in PyTorch are [out_features, in_features]. For `nn.Linear(4096, 4096)`, weight is [4096, 4096]. - q_proj.weight: [4096, 4096] - k_proj.weight: [4096, 4096] - v_proj.weight: [4096, 4096] Fused QKV weight: [12288, 4096]. When transposed for matmul: [4096, 12288]. So I'll pre-process weights in __init__: ```python qkv_w = torch.cat([layer.self_attn.q_proj.weight, layer.self_attn.k_proj.weight, layer.self_attn.v_proj.weight], dim=0) ``` Similarly for MLP, gate and up projections can be fused: - gate_proj.weight: [11008, 4096] - up_proj.weight: [11008, 4096] - Fused: [22016, 4096] Down projection remains separate: [4096, 11008]. Fusing gate+up means one matmul instead of two, then a custom SwiGLU kernel that splits the output. This fusion alone could give meaningful speedup. For the O projection and LM head, they're single matmuls already. For attention, if I can't use SDPA from C++, I'll implement manual attention with `at::matmul` + custom softmax. Alternatively, I can avoid using the cached cos/sin entirely and compute them on-the-fly in the CUDA kernel. This avoids dependency on the reference's caching behavior. Computing inv_freq on the fly: ```cpp for (int i = 0; i < head_dim / 2; i++) { float freq = 1.0f / powf(theta, 2.0f * i / head_dim); // position * freq } ``` But this needs to match PyTorch exactly. PyTorch does: ```python inv_freq = 1.0 / (self.base ** (torch.arange(0, self.dim, 2, dtype=torch.int64).float() / self.dim)) ``` Where `self.dim = head_dim`, `self.base = 10000.0`. Note: `torch.arange(0, self.dim, 2).float()` gives [0, 2, 4, ..., head_dim-2]. Then `inv_freq` has head_dim/2 elements. For position m and dimension pair 2i, 2i+1: ```python freq = m * inv_freq[i] cos_m_i = cos(freq) sin_m_i = sin(freq) ``` Then RoPE application: ```python x1 = x[..., 2*i] x2 = x[..., 2*i+1] rotated[..., 2*i] = x1 * cos_m_i - x2 * sin_m_i rotated[..., 2*i+1] = x2 * cos_m_i + x1 * sin_m_i ``` Wait, there are two common formulations: 1. The original RoPE: [x1, x2] rotates by angle freq - new_x1 = x1*cos - x2*sin - new_x2 = x2*cos + x1*sin 2. The `transformers` implementation might use a different stacking. Let me check the typical `transformers` RoPE implementation: ```python # Apply rotary embeddings def rotate_half(x): x1 = x[..., :x.shape[-1] // 2] x2 = x[..., x.shape[-1] // 2:] return torch.cat([-x2, x1], dim=-1) x_embed = (x * cos) + (rotate_half(x) * sin) ``` Where `cos` and `sin` are of shape [..., seq_len, head_dim], computed by repeating the frequency pairs: ```python freqs = torch.einsum('i,j->ij', t, inv_freq) # [seq_len, head_dim/2] emb = torch.cat([freqs, freqs], dim=-1) # [seq_len, head_dim] cos = emb.cos() sin = emb.sin() ``` So `cos` and `sin` have shape [seq_len, head_dim], where each pair of elements is the same. And `rotate_half` splits in half along the last dimension: - For head_dim=128: - x1 = x[..., :64] - x2 = x[..., 64:] - rotated = [-x2, x1] = [..., -x[..., 64:], x[..., :64]] Wait, that's splitting into two halves and swapping. But the original RoPE paper rotates pairs of adjacent elements. These are different implementations! The `transformers` implementation uses the "half-rotation" approach, not the adjacent-pair approach. I need to match exactly what `transformers` does. For DeepSeek specifically, let me check... Actually in `transformers.models.deepseek.modeling_deepseek`, the rotary embedding is likely inherited from `LlamaRotaryEmbedding` or uses the same pattern. So `rotate_half` does: ``` x1 = x[..., : x.shape[-1] // 2] x2 = x[..., x.shape[-1] // 2 :] return torch.cat((-x2, x1), dim=-1) ``` And `apply_rotary_pos_emb` does: ```python return (x * cos) + (rotate_half(x) * sin) ``` This is what I need to implement in CUDA. cos and sin have shape [1, 1, seq_len, head_dim] or [seq_len, head_dim]. Let me compute these in Python during init: ```python head_dim = config.hidden_size // config.num_attention_heads inv_freq = 1.0 / (10000.0 ** (torch.arange(0, head_dim, 2).float() / head_dim)) t = torch.arange(512, dtype=torch.float32) freqs = torch.outer(t, inv_freq) # [512, head_dim/2] emb = torch.cat([freqs, freqs], dim=-1) # [512, head_dim] cos = emb.cos() sin = emb.sin() ``` Wait, I need to make sure `inv_freq` is computed the same way as `transformers`. Let me trace through: ```python inv_freq = 1.0 / (self.base ** (torch.arange(0, self.dim, 2, dtype=torch.int64).float() / self.dim)) ``` In PyTorch: - `torch.arange(0, head_dim, 2, dtype=torch.int64)` gives [0, 2, 4, ..., head_dim-2] - `.float()` converts to float - `/ head_dim` divides by head_dim - `self.base ** (...)` computes power - `1.0 / (...)` gives 1 / base^(i/head_dim) for i = 0, 2, 4, ... So `inv_freq` has `head_dim/2` elements: `inv_freq[j] = 1 / (base ^ (2j / head_dim))` for j = 0, 1, ..., head_dim/2 - 1. Then `torch.outer(t, inv_freq)` gives `freqs[m, j] = m * inv_freq[j] = m / (base ^ (2j/head_dim))`. Then `emb = torch.cat([freqs, freqs], dim=-1)` gives `emb[m, 2j] = emb[m, 2j+1] = m / (base ^ (2j/head_dim))`. And `cos[m] = cos(emb[m])`, `sin[m] = sin(emb[m])`. Then RoPE: ```python def rotate_half(x): x1 = x[..., : x.shape[-1] // 2] x2 = x[..., x.shape[-1] // 2 :] return torch.cat((-x2, x1), dim=-1) ``` If x has shape [..., head_dim]: - x1 shape: [..., head_dim/2] - x2 shape: [..., head_dim/2] - -x2 shape: [..., head_dim/2] - torch.cat([-x2, x1], dim=-1) shape: [..., head_dim] - `rotate_half(x)` replaces the first half with negated second half, and the second half with first half. Wait, that doesn't seem like a rotation in the geometric sense. Let me verify with an example: x = [a, b, c, d] (head_dim=4) x1 = [a, b] x2 = [c, d] rotate_half(x) = [-c, -d, a, b] Then: new_x = x * cos + rotate_half(x) * sin = [a, b, c, d] * [c1, c2, c3, c4] + [-c, -d, a, b] * [s1, s2, s3, s4] = [a*c1 - c*s1, b*c2 - d*s2, c*c3 + a*s3, d*c4 + b*s4] Hmm, this mixes non-corresponding elements, which seems odd. But this is what `transformers` does. I need to match it exactly, regardless of whether it "makes sense" mathematically. Actually, looking more carefully: In `transformers`, `cos` and `sin` are computed as: ```python emb = torch.cat((freqs, freqs), dim=-1) # [seq_len, head_dim] cos = emb.cos().to(dtype=x.dtype) # [seq_len, head_dim] sin = emb.sin().to(dtype=x.dtype) # [seq_len, head_dim] ``` And `apply_rotary_pos_emb`: ```python cos = cos.unsqueeze(1).unsqueeze(0).expand(batch, heads, seq, head_dim) sin = sin.unsqueeze(1).unsqueeze(0).expand(batch, heads, seq, head_dim) return (x * cos) + (rotate_half(x) * sin) ``` Yes, this is the standard `transformers` RoPE implementation. The reason `rotate_half` works is because the frequency for the second half of dimensions is the same as the first half (since `emb` repeats `freqs`). When you look at the pair-wise rotation, it actually works out because of how the frequencies are repeated. In my CUDA kernel, I need to implement `rotate_half` exactly. Wait, there's another thing. In `transformers`, cos/sin are sometimes cached and sliced to the actual seq_len. So for position m, cos[m] has head_dim elements. And `rotate_half` operates on the entire head_dim. For my CUDA kernel: ```cuda // For each token position m and head // Load cos[m, :] and sin[m, :] into registers // For each element i in head_dim: // x = q[m, head, i] // if i < head_dim / 2: // rotated = -q[m, head, i + head_dim/2] // else: // rotated = q[m, head, i - head_dim/2] // q_out[m, head, i] = x * cos[i] + rotated * sin[i] ``` Wait, that's not right. `rotate_half` doesn't depend on position! It only depends on the element index within the head_dim. Let me trace through for a single head and position: - Input x has shape [head_dim] - rotate_half(x)[i] = -x[i + head_dim/2] if i < head_dim/2, else x[i - head_dim/2] So for m-th position: - For i in [0, head_dim/2): output[i] = x[i] * cos[m, i] + (-x[i + head_dim/2]) * sin[m, i] - For i in [head_dim/2, head_dim): output[i] = x[i] * cos[m, i] + x[i - head_dim/2] * sin[m, i] Yes, this matches the formula. Since `emb` is `[freqs, freqs]`, we have `cos[m, i] = cos[m, i + head_dim/2]` and `sin[m, i] = sin[m, i + head_dim/2]`. But the formula uses the actual index, not paired indices. Wait, actually since `cos` and `sin` are computed from `emb = cat([freqs, freqs])`: - cos[m, 2j] = cos(freqs[m, j]) - cos[m, 2j+1] = cos(freqs[m, j]) - But for `rotate_half`, we're splitting by halves, not pairs. For head_dim=128: - x1 = x[0:64] - x2 = x[64:128] - rotate_half(x) = [-x2, x1] = [-x[64], ..., -x[127], x[0], ..., x[63]] And cos/sin: - cos = [c0, c1, ..., c127] - where c0..c63 are from freqs, and c64..c127 are also from freqs (repeated) So for i=0: output = x[0]*cos[0] + (-x[64])*sin[0] For i=64: output = x[64]*cos[64] + x[0]*sin[64] But cos[64] = cos[0] and sin[64] = sin[0] (since freqs are repeated) So: output[0] = x[0]*c - x[64]*s output[64] = x[64]*c + x[0]*s Which is a standard 2D rotation! The "half" in rotate_half refers to splitting the full dimension in half, and because the second half shares the same frequencies as the first half, it forms rotation pairs: (0, 64), (1, 65), etc. For adjacent-pair RoPE (original paper), the pairs are (0, 1), (2, 3), etc. For `transformers` half-rotation, the pairs are (0, head_dim/2), (1, head_dim/2+1), etc. Both are valid implementations, just with different pairing. I just need to match `transformers`. OK, so my RoPE CUDA kernel can implement this directly without looking up the reference's cos/sin tensors. I'll compute `cos(m * inv_freq[j])` and `sin(m * inv_freq[j])` on the fly for each position m and frequency j, where j ranges from 0 to head_dim/2 - 1. For position m and pair j: - i1 = j - i2 = j + head_dim/2 - c = cos(m * inv_freq[j]) - s = sin(m * inv_freq[j]) - out[i1] = x[i1] * c - x[i2] * s - out[i2] = x[i2] * c + x[i1] * s And `inv_freq[j] = 1.0f / powf(10000.0f, 2.0f * j / head_dim)` To match PyTorch exactly: - PyTorch computes `inv_freq` in fp64 or fp32 depending on implementation - For Llama/DeepSeek rotary embeddings, `inv_freq` is typically fp32 - The `torch.outer(t, inv_freq)` is fp32 - `torch.cos/sin` on fp32 input gives fp32 output - Then cast to bf16 So in my kernel, I should compute inv_freq in fp32, freq in fp32, cos/sin in fp32, then cast to bf16 for the final multiplication. Computing inv_freq on-the-fly in the kernel: For each head and position, I need to know `inv_freq[j]`. Computing `powf` for every element is expensive. It's better to precompute `inv_freq` and pass it to the kernel, or precompute cos/sin for all positions. Given seq_len is fixed at 512, precomputing cos/sin as [512, head_dim] tensors and passing them to the kernel is the most efficient approach. And I can compute them in Python to exactly match PyTorch. In Python: ```python head_dim = 4096 // 32 # 128 inv_freq = 1.0 / (10000.0 ** (torch.arange(0, head_dim, 2).float() / head_dim)) t = torch.arange(512, dtype=torch.float32) freqs = torch.einsum('i,j->ij', t, inv_freq) # [512, 64] emb = torch.cat([freqs, freqs], dim=-1) # [512, 128] cos = emb.cos().to(torch.bfloat16) sin = emb.sin().to(torch.bfloat16) ``` Wait, PyTorch's RoPE might compute `torch.outer(t, inv_freq)` not `einsum`. But mathematically they're the same. To be absolutely sure, let me compute using the exact same formula as `transformers`: ```python inv_freq = 1.0 / (self.base ** (torch.arange(0, self.dim, 2, dtype=torch.int64).float() / self.dim)) ``` In Python, I'll copy this exactly. Actually, let me just extract the cos/sin from the model directly: ```python with torch.no_grad(): dummy = torch.zeros((1, 1), dtype=torch.long, device=model.device) _ = model(dummy) # Run once to initialize caches ``` Then access `model.model.layers[0].self_attn.rotary_emb.cos_cached` and `sin_cached`. I should check and slice appropriately. Actually, it's easier to just compute it myself using the exact formula. I'll verify my computation produces the same values as the reference. Alright, I think I have a solid plan. Now let me write the actual code. Actually, I want to think about one more optimization: buffer reuse. In my C++ forward, I can avoid creating new tensors for every intermediate result by reusing pre-allocated buffers. This saves memory allocation overhead. But with `at::matmul`, the output is always a new tensor. To reuse buffers, I'd need to use `at::matmul_out` or similar. PyTorch's C++ API has `at::mm_out` for matrix multiplication with an output tensor. Using `at::mm_out` allows buffer reuse. For example: ```cpp auto qkv = torch::empty({batch_seq, qkv_out_dim}, hidden.options()); at::mm_out(qkv, hidden, qkv_weight); ``` This avoids allocating a new tensor for the QKV output. Similarly for other matmuls. For the SwiGLU intermediate, I can allocate a buffer once and reuse it across layers. This could provide meaningful speedup by avoiding the PyTorch allocator. However, `at::mm_out` requires the output tensor to have the correct shape, which is fine. For attention, the QK^T scores can also use a pre-allocated buffer. Let me plan the buffer shapes (for batch=4, seq=512): - hidden: [2048, 4096] - qkv_fused: [2048, 12288] - q, k, v (views of qkv_fused): each [2048, 4096] - Actually, after QKV matmul, reshape to [4, 512, 32, 128] then permute to [4, 32, 512, 128] - attn_scores: [128, 512, 512] (for QK^T, batch=4*32=128) - attn_weights: [128, 512, 512] - attn_output: [128, 512, 128] - o_proj_output: [2048, 4096] - mlp_fused: [2048, 22016] - mlp_activated: [2048, 11008] - mlp_output: [2048, 4096] - logits: [2048, vocab_size] For buffer reuse across layers, the intermediate activations have fixed sizes. I can pre-allocate them once. For RoPE application, I can apply it in-place to q and k buffers. For the residual adds, I can do them in-place on the hidden buffer. RMS Norm can be done in-place as well, or to a new buffer. Actually, doing everything in-place is tricky because we need the original hidden for the residual add. So: 1. Save hidden_copy = hidden.clone() (or hidden itself if we compute norm to a separate buffer) 2. Compute normed = rms_norm(hidden) → new buffer 3. Compute qkv = matmul(normed, qkv_w) → new buffer 4. ... 5. Compute attn_out = matmul(attn, o_w) → new buffer 6. hidden = hidden_copy + attn_out → in-place add into hidden_copy, or new buffer To minimize allocations, I'll use `torch::empty` to create buffers once, then use `at::copy_`, `at::add_out`, `at::mm_out`, and custom kernels that write to output buffers. This adds complexity to the C++ code but should be faster. For the first implementation, I'll use the simpler approach with `at::` functions creating new tensors, and only optimize if needed. Actually, let me think about the compilation time. `load_inline` compiles the CUDA code each time the module is loaded. For a complex kernel with many lines, compilation might take 1-2 minutes. Alright, let me try to write the custom implementation. I'll keep it as correct as possible. One concern: I said I'll use `at::matmul` for GEMMs. But the PyTorch reference uses `nn.Linear`, which calls `F.linear`. `F.linear` computes `input @ weight.t() + bias`. For no bias, this is `input @ weight.t()`. If I pre-transpose weights and use `at::matmul(input, weight_t)`, this is exactly equivalent to `F.linear(input, weight, None)`. But internally, `F.linear` might use `addmm` or `mm` depending on the input shape, with different algorithms. For [2048, 4096] @ [4096, 12288], it will call into `mm` or `addmm` which uses cuBLAS. So `at::matmul` should be equivalent. Wait, `matmul` does broadcasting and might take a different code path than `linear`. For 2D inputs, they should both call `addmm`. To be safer, I can use `at::mm` directly: ```cpp auto qkv = at::mm(hidden, qkv_weight_t); ``` `at::mm` is the raw matrix multiply for 2D tensors, exactly what `F.linear` calls internally. For batched attention matmuls (QK^T and PV), I need batched matrix multiply. `at::matmul` handles the batching. `at::bmm` is another option. For QK^T: - query shape: [128, 512, 128] - key shape: [128, 512, 128] - After transpose: [128, 128, 512] - `at::bmm(query, key.transpose(1, 2))` gives [128, 512, 512] Actually, PyTorch's attention might use `einsum` or `matmul`. For `matmul` with 3D tensors: - [128, 512, 128] @ [128, 128, 512] = [128, 512, 512] This requires the key to be transposed to [128, 128, 512]. `key.transpose(-2, -1)` gives [128, 128, 512] if key is [128, 512, 128]. Then `at::matmul(query, key.transpose(-2, -1))` works. For the PV matmul: - attn_weights: [128, 512, 512] - value: [128, 512, 128] - `at::matmul(attn_weights, value)` gives [128, 512, 128] This matches. Let me now write the code. I'll structure it as follows: **Python side:** ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM, AutoConfig # CUDA/C++ source cuda_source = r''' #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <math.h> // ... CUDA kernels ... torch::Tensor deepseek_forward( torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor embed_weight, torch::Tensor lm_head_weight, torch::Tensor cos, torch::Tensor sin, std::vector<torch::Tensor> norm1_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> qkv_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> o_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> norm2_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> mlp_fused_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> mlp_down_weights, torch::Tensor final_norm_weight, float eps) { // Implementation } ''' # Compile deepseek_cuda = torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline( name="deepseek_cuda", cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=["deepseek_forward"], extra_cuda_cflags=["-O3"], verbose=False ) class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, model_name, config): super().__init__() self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config) self.model.eval() self.config = config # Precompute RoPE head_dim = config.hidden_size // config.num_attention_heads inv_freq = 1.0 / (10000.0 ** (torch.arange(0, head_dim, 2, dtype=torch.float32) / head_dim)) t = torch.arange(512, dtype=torch.float32) freqs = torch.outer(t, inv_freq) emb = torch.cat([freqs, freqs], dim=-1) self.register_buffer('cos', emb.cos().to(torch.bfloat16), persistent=False) self.register_buffer('sin', emb.sin().to(torch.bfloat16), persistent=False) # Extract weights self._extract_weights() def _extract_weights(self): self.embed_weight = self.model.model.embed_tokens.weight.to(torch.bfloat16) self.lm_head_weight = self.model.lm_head.weight.to(torch.bfloat16) self.norm1_weights = [] self.qkv_weights = [] self.o_weights = [] self.norm2_weights = [] self.mlp_fused_weights = [] self.mlp_down_weights = [] for layer in self.model.model.layers: # Norms self.norm1_weights.append(layer.input_layernorm.weight.to(torch.bfloat16)) self.norm2_weights.append(layer.post_attention_layernorm.weight.to(torch.bfloat16)) # Fused QKV q_w = layer.self_attn.q_proj.weight k_w = layer.self_attn.k_proj.weight v_w = layer.self_attn.v_proj.weight qkv_w = torch.cat([q_w, k_w, v_w], dim=0).t().contiguous().to(torch.bfloat16) self.qkv_weights.append(qkv_w) # O proj o_w = layer.self_attn.o_proj.weight.t().contiguous().to(torch.bfloat16) self.o_weights.append(o_w) # Fused MLP gate+up gate_w = layer.mlp.gate_proj.weight up_w = layer.mlp.up_proj.weight mlp_fused = torch.cat([gate_w, up_w], dim=0).t().contiguous().to(torch.bfloat16) self.mlp_fused_weights.append(mlp_fused) # MLP down down_w = layer.mlp.down_proj.weight.t().contiguous().to(torch.bfloat16) self.mlp_down_weights.append(down_w) self.final_norm_weight = self.model.model.norm.weight.to(torch.bfloat16) def forward(self, x): return deepseek_cuda.deepseek_forward( x, self.embed_weight, self.lm_head_weight, self.cos, self.sin, self.norm1_weights, self.qkv_weights, self.o_weights, self.norm2_weights, self.mlp_fused_weights, self.mlp_down_weights, self.final_norm_weight, self.config.rms_norm_eps ) ``` This looks reasonable. For the C++ side, I'll implement the forward pass. Let me think about the C++ implementation in detail: ```cpp torch::Tensor deepseek_forward( torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor embed_weight, torch::Tensor lm_head_weight, torch::Tensor cos, torch::Tensor sin, std::vector<torch::Tensor> norm1_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> qkv_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> o_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> norm2_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> mlp_fused_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> mlp_down_weights, torch::Tensor final_norm_weight, float eps ) { int batch_size = input_ids.size(0); int seq_len = input_ids.size(1); int hidden_size = embed_weight.size(1); int num_heads = 32; // or pass as param int head_dim = hidden_size / num_heads; int num_layers = norm1_weights.size(); int vocab_size = embed_weight.size(0); // Embedding auto hidden = at::embedding(embed_weight, input_ids); hidden = hidden.reshape({batch_size * seq_len, hidden_size}); auto causal_mask = torch::triu(torch::ones({seq_len, seq_len}, torch::TensorOptions().dtype(torch::kBool).device(input_ids.device())), 1); for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { // Save residual auto residual = hidden; // RMS Norm 1 hidden = rms_norm_cuda(hidden, norm1_weights[layer], eps); // QKV auto qkv = at::mm(hidden, qkv_weights[layer]); // Split QKV auto q = qkv.slice(1, 0, hidden_size); auto k = qkv.slice(1, hidden_size, 2 * hidden_size); auto v = qkv.slice(1, 2 * hidden_size, 3 * hidden_size); // Reshape to [batch, num_heads, seq, head_dim] q = q.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); k = k.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); v = v.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); // RoPE q = apply_rope_cuda(q, cos, sin); k = apply_rope_cuda(k, cos, sin); // Attention auto scores = at::matmul(q, k.transpose(-2, -1)) / std::sqrt(static_cast<float>(head_dim)); scores = scores.masked_fill(causal_mask, -INFINITY); auto attn_weights = at::softmax(scores, -1); auto attn_output = at::matmul(attn_weights, v); // Reshape back attn_output = attn_output.permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).reshape({batch_size * seq_len, hidden_size}); // O proj auto o_out = at::mm(attn_output, o_weights[layer]); // Residual hidden = residual + o_out; // Save residual residual = hidden; // RMS Norm 2 hidden = rms_norm_cuda(hidden, norm2_weights[layer], eps); // MLP fused auto mlp_fused = at::mm(hidden, mlp_fused_weights[layer]); // SwiGLU int intermediate_size = mlp_fused_weights[layer].size(1) / 2; auto gate = mlp_fused.slice(1, 0, intermediate_size); auto up = mlp_fused.slice(1, intermediate_size, 2 * intermediate_size); auto mlp_activated = swiglu_cuda(gate, up); // Down proj auto mlp_out = at::mm(mlp_activated, mlp_down_weights[layer]); // Residual hidden = residual + mlp_out; } // Final norm hidden = rms_norm_cuda(hidden, final_norm_weight, eps); // LM head auto logits = at::mm(hidden, lm_head_weight.t()); logits = logits.to(torch::kFloat32); logits = logits.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, vocab_size}); return logits; } ``` Wait, I notice a few issues: 1. `q, k, v` slicing from QKV: the QKV tensor is [batch_seq, 3*hidden_size]. OK, here's my final structure for the C++ function: ```cpp torch::Tensor deepseek_forward( torch::Tensor input_ids, torch::Tensor embed_weight, torch::Tensor lm_head_weight, torch::Tensor cos, torch::Tensor sin, std::vector<torch::Tensor> norm1_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> qkv_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> o_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> norm2_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> mlp_fused_weights, std::vector<torch::Tensor> mlp_down_weights, torch::Tensor final_norm_weight, float eps) { at::NoGradGuard no_grad; int batch_size = input_ids.size(0); int seq_len = input_ids.size(1); int hidden_size = embed_weight.size(1); int num_heads = 32; int head_dim = hidden_size / num_heads; int num_layers = norm1_weights.size(); int vocab_size = embed_weight.size(0); // Embedding auto hidden = at::embedding(embed_weight, input_ids); hidden = hidden.reshape({batch_size * seq_len, hidden_size}); for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) { auto residual = hidden; // RMSNorm auto x_fp32 = hidden.to(torch::kFloat32); auto variance = x_fp32.pow(2).mean(-1, true); auto normed_fp32 = x_fp32 * torch::rsqrt(variance + eps); auto weight_expanded = norm1_weights[layer].unsqueeze(0).to(torch::kFloat32); hidden = (normed_fp32 * weight_expanded).to(hidden.dtype()); // QKV auto qkv = at::mm(hidden, qkv_weights[layer]); // Split and reshape auto q = qkv.slice(1, 0, hidden_size).reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); auto k = qkv.slice(1, hidden_size, 2 * hidden_size).reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); auto v = qkv.slice(1, 2 * hidden_size, 3 * hidden_size).reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}); // RoPE (custom kernel) apply_rope_cuda(q, k, cos, sin); // Attention auto attn_output = at::scaled_dot_product_attention(q, k, v, c10::nullopt, 0.0, true); // Reshape and O proj attn_output = attn_output.permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).reshape({batch_size * seq_len, hidden_size}); auto o_out = at::mm(attn_output, o_weights[layer]); // Residual hidden = residual + o_out; residual = hidden; // RMSNorm 2 x_fp32 = hidden.to(torch::kFloat32); variance = x_fp32.pow(2).mean(-1, true); normed_fp32 = x_fp32 * torch::rsqrt(variance + eps); weight_expanded = norm2_weights[layer].unsqueeze(0).to(torch::kFloat32); hidden = (normed_fp32 * weight_expanded).to(hidden.dtype()); // MLP fused auto mlp_fused = at::mm(hidden, mlp_fused_weights[layer]); int intermediate_size = mlp_fused_weights[layer].size(1) / 2; // SwiGLU auto gate = mlp_fused.slice(1, 0, intermediate_size); auto up = mlp_fused.slice(1, intermediate_size, 2 * intermediate_size); auto activated = at::silu(gate) * up; // Down proj auto mlp_out = at::mm(activated, mlp_down_weights[layer]); // Residual hidden = residual + mlp_out; } // Final norm auto x_fp32 = hidden.to(torch::kFloat32); auto variance = x_fp32.pow(2).mean(-1, true); auto normed_fp32 = x_fp32 * torch::rsqrt(variance + eps); auto weight_expanded = final_norm_weight.unsqueeze(0).to(torch::kFloat32); hidden = (normed_fp32 * weight_expanded).to(hidden.dtype()); // LM head auto logits = at::mm(hidden, lm_head_weight.t()); logits = logits.to(torch::kFloat32); logits = logits.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, vocab_size}); return logits; } ``` For the RoPE CUDA kernel, I need to handle the `transformers` style half-rotation. Actually, I said I'd use `at::scaled_dot_product_attention`. Let me think about whether I need to include any special headers for it. If I include `torch/extension.h`, it should be available through the `at::` namespace. Wait, but I also need to handle the possibility that `at::scaled_dot_product_attention` might not dispatch correctly for bf16 on H100. Or it might not exist in the PyTorch version. Let me think about this... If it doesn't compile, I'll switch to manual attention. But let me try it first. For the RoPE custom kernel, the CUDA code should be something like: ```cuda __global__ void apply_rope_kernel( at::BFloat16* __restrict__ q, at::BFloat16* __restrict__ k, const at::BFloat16* __restrict__ cos, const at::BFloat16* __restrict__ sin, int batch_size, int num_heads, int seq_len, int head_dim) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int total = batch_size * num_heads * seq_len * head_dim; if (idx >= total) return; // Compute indices int d = idx % head_dim; int s = (idx / head_dim) % seq_len; int h = (idx / (head_dim * seq_len)) % num_heads; int b = idx / (head_dim * seq_len * num_heads); // Determine pair for rotate_half int pair_d = (d < head_dim / 2) ? d + head_dim / 2 : d - head_dim / 2; int pair_idx = b * num_heads * seq_len * head_dim + h * seq_len * head_dim + s * head_dim + pair_d; float x_q = __bfloat162float(q[idx]); float x_k = __bfloat162float(k[idx]); float x_q_pair = __bfloat162float(q[pair_idx]); float x_k_pair = __bfloat162float(k[pair_idx]); float c = __bfloat162float(cos[s * head_dim + d]); float s_val = __bfloat162float(sin[s * head_dim + d]); float rotated_q = (d < head_dim / 2) ? -x_q_pair : x_q_pair; float rotated_k = (d < head_dim / 2) ? -x_k_pair : x_k_pair; float out_q = x_q * c + rotated_q * s_val; float out_k = x_k * c + rotated_k * s_val; q[idx] = __float2bfloat16(out_q); k[idx] = __float2bfloat16(out_k); } ``` Wait, there's a subtle bug. `pair_idx` for the same `b, h, s` but different `d` accesses a different memory location. For each `idx`, I load `q[idx]` and `q[pair_idx]`. But when another thread processes `pair_idx`, it will load `q[pair_idx]` and `q[idx]`. This means each pair is loaded twice, which is wasteful but correct. However, there's a race condition concern: we read from `q` and write to `q` in the same kernel. Different threads might be reading and writing overlapping pairs. But since each pair element is only written by one thread (the thread with `idx = ...`), and each thread reads `q[pair_idx]` which is written by a different thread in the same kernel launch, this IS a race condition! Thread A (d=0) reads q[0] and q[64], writes q[0] and k[0]. Thread B (d=64) reads q[64] and q[0], writes q[64] and k[64]. Thread A reads q[0] before thread B writes it (since both are in the same kernel). But thread A reads the original value, which is fine because q[64] is also the original value (neither has been written yet). Since all threads read all source values before writing any output, and all source values are from the input (not from other threads' outputs), there's no race condition... assuming the GPU executes in a way that all reads complete before writes. But CUDA doesn't guarantee this for arbitrary thread scheduling. Actually, in CUDA, threads within a warp execute simultaneously, and memory operations are not necessarily ordered across warps. However, for this specific case: - Thread A and thread B might be in different warps - Thread A could read q[64], then thread B writes q[64], then thread A writes q[0] - But thread A doesn't read q[64] again after writing q[0] Since the source values are from the original array, and we're writing to different locations than we're reading (except that another thread might read what we write), there's a potential read-after-write hazard between warps. To fix this, I should use separate output arrays: ```cuda q_input[idx] -> compute -> q_output[idx] k_input[idx] -> compute -> k_output[idx] ``` Or process in-place carefully. Let me write it with in-place but ensure correctness by only using original values. Actually, the safest approach is to process each pair of elements (d and d + head_dim/2) in a single thread, and write both outputs. This way, there's no cross-thread dependency. But then each thread processes 2 elements, so I need fewer threads. Let me redesign: - Total elements = batch * heads * seq * head_dim - Launch batch * heads * seq * (head_dim / 2) threads - Each thread handles one pair (d, d + head_dim/2) ```cuda __global__ void apply_rope_kernel(...) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int num_pairs = batch_size * num_heads * seq_len * (head_dim / 2); if (idx >= num_pairs) return; int pair_idx = idx % (head_dim / 2); int s = (idx / (head_dim / 2)) % seq_len; int h = (idx / (seq_len * head_dim / 2)) % num_heads; int b = idx / (num_heads * seq_len * head_dim / 2); int base = ((b * num_heads + h) * seq_len + s) * head_dim; int d1 = base + pair_idx; int d2 = base + pair_idx + head_dim / 2; float x1_q = __bfloat162float(q[d1]); float x2_q = __bfloat162float(q[d2]); float x1_k = __bfloat162float(k[d1]); float x2_k = __bfloat162float(k[d2]); float c = __bfloat162float(cos[s * head_dim + pair_idx]); // Note: cos has repeated values float s_val = __bfloat162float(sin[s * head_dim + pair_idx]); q[d1] = __float2bfloat16(x1_q * c - x2_q * s_val); q[d2] = __float2bfloat16(x2_q * c + x1_q * s_val); k[d1] = __float2bfloat16(x1_k * c - x2_k * s_val); k[d2] = __float2bfloat16(x2_k * c + x1_k * s_val); } ``` This is clean and race-free. Each thread writes two elements, and there are no overlapping writes. Wait, but in the `transformers` implementation, `rotate_half` splits by the middle, not by adjacent pairs. For head_dim=128: - x1 = x[..., :64] - x2 = x[..., 64:] Then `rotate_half(x)` = [-x2, x1] = concatenation of negated second half and first half. And: ```python (x * cos) + (rotate_half(x) * sin) ``` For element d1 = pair_idx (in [0, 63]): - out[d1] = x[d1] * cos[d1] + rotate_half(x)[d1] * sin[d1] - rotate_half(x)[d1] = -x[d1 + 64] (since it's the first element of the negated x2) - So out[d1] = x[d1] * cos[d1] - x[d1 + 64] * sin[d1] For element d2 = pair_idx + 64 (in [64, 127]): - out[d2] = x[d2] * cos[d2] + rotate_half(x)[d2] * sin[d2] - rotate_half(x)[d2] = x[d2 - 64] (since it's an element of x1 shifted by 64) - cos[d2] = cos[d1] and sin[d2] = sin[d1] (since freqs are repeated) - So out[d2] = x[d2] * cos[d1] + x[d1] * sin[d1] Yes, my kernel formulas are correct! For the `cos` and `sin` indexing: - `cos[s * head_dim + pair_idx]`: but cos has shape [seq_len, head_dim], so `cos[s, pair_idx]` - For `d2`, we use the same cos/sin values as for `d1` because `cos[s, d2] = cos[s, d1]` (repeated freqs) My kernel reads `cos[s * head_dim + pair_idx]`. But wait, if `cos` is stored as [seq_len, head_dim], then for position s and dimension pair_idx, the index is `s * head_dim + pair_idx`. For dimension `d2 = pair_idx + head_dim/2`, the index would be `s * head_dim + pair_idx + head_dim/2`. But since `cos[:, :head_dim/2] == cos[:, head_dim/2:]` (both are `torch.cat([freqs, freqs])`), these two indices have the same value. In the reference, when RoPE is applied: ```python cos = cos.unsqueeze(1).unsqueeze(0).expand(batch_size, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim) ``` So `cos` has shape [batch, heads, seq, head_dim], but it's expanded from [1, 1, seq, head_dim]. For my kernel, I'll pass `cos` and `sin` as 2D tensors [seq_len, head_dim], and each thread uses the appropriate row. Wait, but the `transformers` RoPE might have `cos` with shape [1, 1, seq, head_dim] or [1, seq, 1, head_dim] depending on broadcasting. The actual values are the same. In my kernel, I'll just use `cos[s * head_dim + pair_idx]`. Alright, let me also think about whether to write a custom RMS Norm kernel or use `at::` ops. Using `at::` ops is safer for correctness. Let me finalize the code: ```cuda #include <torch/extension.h> #include <cuda_runtime.h> #include <cuda_bf16.h> __global__ void apply_rope_kernel( __nv_bfloat16* q, __nv_bfloat16* k, const __nv_bfloat16* cos, const __nv_bfloat16* sin, int batch_size, int num_heads, int seq_len, int head_dim) { int idx = blockIdx.x * blockDim.x + threadIdx.x; int num_pairs = batch_size * num_heads * seq_len * (head_dim / 2); if (idx >= num_pairs) return; int pair_idx = idx % (head_dim / 2); int s = (idx / (head_dim / 2)) % seq_len; int h = (idx / (seq_len * head_dim / 2)) % num_heads; int b = idx / (num_heads * seq_len * head_dim / 2); int base = ((b * num_heads + h) * seq_len + s) * head_dim; int d1 = base + pair_idx; int d2 = base + pair_idx + head_dim / 2; float x1_q = __bfloat162float(q[d1]); float x2_q = __bfloat162float(q[d2]); float x1_k = __bfloat162float(k[d1]); float x2_k = __bfloat162float(k[d2]); float c = __bfloat162float(cos[s * head_dim + pair_idx]); float s_val = __bfloat162float(sin[s * head_dim + pair_idx]); } torch::Tensor apply_rope_cuda(torch::Tensor q, torch::Tensor k, torch::Tensor cos, torch::Tensor sin) { int batch_size = q.size(0); int num_heads = q.size(1); int seq_len = q.size(2); int head_dim = q.size(3); int num_pairs = batch_size * num_heads * seq_len * (head_dim / 2); int threads = 256; int blocks = (num_pairs + threads - 1) / threads; apply_rope_kernel<<<blocks, threads>>>( reinterpret_cast<__nv_bfloat16*>(q.data_ptr()), reinterpret_cast<__nv_bfloat16*>(k.data_ptr()), reinterpret_cast<__nv_bfloat16*>(cos.data_ptr()), reinterpret_cast<__nv_bfloat16*>(sin.data_ptr()), batch_size, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim ); return q; // in-place modification } torch::Tensor deepseek_forward(...) { ... } ``` Wait, there are some issues with this: 1. `q.data_ptr()` returns a `void*`. Let me implement manual eager attention with exact matching: ```cpp // QK^T auto scores = at::matmul(q, k.transpose(-2, -1)); scores = scores / std::sqrt(static_cast<float>(head_dim)); // Causal mask auto causal_mask_bool = torch::triu(torch::ones({seq_len, seq_len}, torch::TensorOptions().dtype(torch::kBool).device(q.device())), 1); scores = scores.masked_fill(causal_mask_bool, -std::numeric_limits<float>::infinity()); // Softmax in fp32 auto scores_fp32 = scores.to(torch::kFloat32); auto attn_weights = at::softmax(scores_fp32, -1); attn_weights = attn_weights.to(q.dtype()); // PV auto attn_output = at::matmul(attn_weights, v); ``` Wait, there are a few issues: 1. `k.transpose(-2, -1)` on a 4D tensor [B, H, S, D] gives [B, H, D, S]. 2. `at::matmul(q, k_t)` where q is [B, H, S, D] and k_t is [B, H, D, S] gives [B, H, S, S]. Now, let me think about whether there are any other sources of numerical difference. 1. **Weight layout**: The reference uses `nn.Linear` with its own weight layout. However, the memory access pattern differs, which shouldn't affect result values for exact matmul algorithms (though floating point is non-associative, different accumulation orders can give slightly different results). But cuBLAS's algorithm selection might differ between `nn.Linear` (which calls `addmm` with specific settings) and my `at::mm`. This could lead to small numerical differences due to different accumulation orders. PyTorch 2.0+ uses deterministic algorithms by default in some modes, but generally, floating point matmuls can vary by small amounts depending on tile sizes and parallel reductions. For bf16 Tensor Cores, the accumulation is in fp32, but different tiling strategies might sum in different orders, causing ±1 ULP differences. Over 30 layers, these differences could accumulate. But the tolerance is 1e-4, which for typical logits (range maybe -10 to 10) means absolute error up to 0.0001. A 1 ULP difference in bf16 is ~0.01 for values around 1, or larger for larger values. Wait, bf16 has 7 bits of mantissa, so precision is about 1/128 ≈ 0.008 for values around 1.0. For values around 10.0, precision is about 0.08. If the reference logits are around 10, a 1 ULP difference is 0.08, which is LARGER than the absolute tolerance of 0.0001! Wait, that can't be right. Let me recalculate. bf16 has: - 1 sign bit - 8 exponent bits - 7 mantissa bits The unit in the last place (ULP) for a value x is approximately x * 2^(-7) = x / 128. For x = 10, ULP ≈ 10/128 ≈ 0.078. For x = 1, ULP ≈ 1/128 ≈ 0.0078. So a single ULP difference in a bf16 value can be as large as ~0.08 for logits around 10. This is much larger than the tolerance of 1e-4 (0.0001). But wait, we're returning fp32 logits. The tolerance is on the fp32 comparison, not on bf16 intermediates. If the reference computes in bf16 and casts to fp32, and I compute in bf16 and cast to fp32, a 1 ULP difference in bf16 will show up as a ~0.08 difference in fp32. This is WAY beyond the 1e-4 tolerance! This means I need EXACT bf16 computation, not approximate. The accumulation of 1 ULP differences across 30 layers could easily cause errors much larger than 1e-4. This is a CRITICAL insight. For strict tolerance like 1e-4 on the final fp32 output, every single operation must match the reference exactly. With bf16 precision and 30 layers, small differences compound. But wait, I think I miscalculated. The tolerance is `atol=rtol=1e-4`. For fp32, this means: - `rtol=1e-4`: relative error of 0.01%. For a logit of 10, absolute error can be 10 * 1e-4 = 0.001. - `atol=1e-4`: absolute error of 0.0001. So the tolerance is `max(atol, rtol * |expected|) = max(0.0001, 0.0001 * |expected|)`. For |expected| = 10, tolerance = max(0.0001, 0.001) = 0.001. For |expected| = 1, tolerance = max(0.0001, 0.0001) = 0.0001. If there's a 1 ULP difference in bf16 at some intermediate layer, the resulting logit might differ by more than 0.001. This is concerning. But actually, typical LLM logits have a very skewed distribution. The maximum logit might be around 10-20, but most are smaller. And the tolerance uses element-wise comparison. So some elements might exceed tolerance while others don't. For strict correctness, I need the computation to match the reference very closely. One source of difference: if the reference uses cuDNN/cuBLAS algorithm A for matmul, and I use algorithm B, the results can differ by 1-2 ULPs in bf16. But does the reference really use SDPA and cuBLAS? Yes, which means it's using highly optimized kernels. My `at::mm` will also call cuBLAS, and PyTorch's dispatcher might select the same algorithm for the same shapes. However, the `transformers` library's `nn.Linear` uses `F.linear` which might call `addmm` instead of `mm`. `addmm` can use a different code path than `mm`, potentially with different accumulation order. For `addmm(input, mat1, mat2, *, beta=1, alpha=1)`, the operation is `beta * input + alpha * (mat1 @ mat2)`. For `beta=1, alpha=1`. If `input` is zero (for linear layers without bias), `addmm` computes `0 + mat1 @ mat2`. But internally, PyTorch might still call a different cuBLAS routine (e.g., `cublasGemmEx` with C initially zeroed, vs `cublasGemmStridedBatchedEx` for `mm` of specific shapes). The numerical result might be the same algorithmically but could differ in practice due to: 1. Different tile sizes 2. Different parallel reduction schemes 3. Whether the output tensor is zeroed or accumulated For exact matching, using the same PyTorch ops as the reference is safest. causal_mask = torch::triu(\n torch::ones({seq_len, seq_len}, torch::dtype(torch::kBool).device(input_ids.device())), \n 1);\n \n for (int layer = 0; layer < num_layers; layer++) {\n auto residual = weight_expanded = norm1_weights[layer].unsqueeze(0).to(torch::kFloat32);\n hidden = (normed_fp32 * weight_expanded).to(hidden.dtype());\n \n auto q = at::linear(hidden, q_weights[layer]);\n auto k = at::linear(hidden, k_weights[layer]);\n auto v = at::linear(hidden, v_weights[layer]);\n \n q = q.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous();\n k = k.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous();\n v = v.reshape({batch_size, seq_len, num_heads, head_dim}).permute({0, 2, 1, 3}).contiguous();\n \n int k.transpose(-2, -1));\n scores = scores / std::sqrt(static_cast<float>(head_dim));\n auto scores_fp32 = scores.to(torch::kFloat32);\n scores_fp32 = scores_fp32.masked_fill(causal_mask, at::linear(hidden, up_weights[layer]);\n auto activated = at::silu(gate) * up;\n \n auto mlp_out = at::linear(activated, down_weights[layer]);\n \n hidden = residual + mlp_out;\n }\n \n auto x_fp32 = My manual forward: ```python def forward(self, input_ids): x = self.embed_tokens(input_ids) # x: (batch, seq_len, hidden) all_cos = self.cos[:seq_len] # already 2D if precomputed correctly all_sin = self.sin[:seq_len] for i in range(num_layers): residual = x x = rmsnorm(x, norm1_w) qkv = F.linear(x, self.qkv_weights[i]) # (bs, seq, 3*hidden) q, k, v = qkv.split(hidden_size, dim=-1) q = q.view(bs, seq, num_heads, head_dim).transpose(1,2) k = k.view(bs, seq, num_heads, head_dim).transpose(1,2) v = v.view(bs, seq, num_heads, head_dim).transpose(1,2) apply_rope(q, k, all_cos, all_sin) # attention scores = torch.matmul(q, k.transpose(-2,-1)) / sqrt(head_dim) scores = scores + self.causal_mask # mask should be (1,1,seq,seq) attn = F.softmax(scores, dim=-1, dtype=torch.float32).to(q.dtype) out = torch.matmul(attn, v).transpose(1,2).reshape(bs, seq, hidden) x = residual + F.linear(out, o_weights[i]) residual = x x = rmsnorm(x, norm2_w) gateup = F.linear(x, self.gateup_weights[i]) gate, up = gateup.split(intermediate_size, dim=-1) activated = F.silu(gate) * up x = residual + F.linear(activated, down_weights[i]) x = rmsnorm(x, final_norm_w) logits = F.linear(x, lm_head_weight) return logits ``` For rmsnorm, I can define a function: ```python def rmsnorm(x, weight, eps): x_float = x.float() norm = x_float * torch.rsqrt(x_float.pow(2).mean(-1, keepdim=True) + eps) return (norm * weight).to(x.dtype) ``` For RoPE, a simple element-wise kernel or just a PyTorch implementation: ```python def apply_rope(q, k, cos, sin): # q,k: (bs, num_heads, seq, head_dim) # cos,sin: (seq, head_dim) q_embed = (q * cos) + (rotate_half(q) * sin) ... ``` where `rotate_half` is `torch.cat([-x[..., head_dim//2:], x[..., :head_dim//2]], dim=-1)`. This is pure PyTorch and should compile/run fine. It's likely slower than a fused kernel but much faster than the reference because: - The reference uses dynamic graphs, many temporary tensors, and the transformers library is not optimized for pure inference compared to a streamlined manual implementation. - Still, the reference is already using `torch.nn.Linear` which is just `matmul` too. However, the reference may include extra copies, checks, and calculate RoPE on the fly inside the attention module. My code precomputes and fuses QKV. Actually, there might be a heavy cost from doing everything in pure Python (multiple Python operations) vs the reference doing a compiled forward. But transformers library forward is also Python loop over layers. So my version should be similar or faster because of fused QKV and MLP weights and precomputed RoPE. But to make it significantly faster, maybe I should also use `torch.jit.script` or `torch.compile`? `torch.compile` on an 7B model with 32 layers might take very long to compile and will likely exceed tool time limits. Better keep it as plain torch. Also, to reduce overhead, I should call `.contiguous()` on q, k after RoPE to ensure `matmul` gets contiguous inputs. Though the default transpose makes it non-contiguous; but `matmul` may handle it. Wait, another idea: during initialization, transpose the weight matrices to (in, out) so that we can use `torch.matmul` directly with the transposed weight without needing the implicit transpose in linear (which actually transposes at kernel launch with no copy). That's a micro-optimization. Also, when using `F.linear`, you pass weight of shape (out, in). Internally, it does `input @ weight.T`. So the weight is read transposed. That's fine. For 7B model, the extra memory from concatenation is: - QKV concat: original has 3 * (4096*4096) = 50M params ~200MB. New tensor is same 200MB, not double; `torch.cat` creates a new contiguous tensor, so it does duplicate memory for these weights. For a 7B model, total weights ~7GB in bf16. The extra copy for QKV (~200MB) and MLP (~220MB) is small compared to 7GB. Acceptable. Wait, actually, 4096*4096*3 = 50,331,648 params. In bf16 that's 96.7 MB. Not a big deal. callcompile_kernel { "kernel_code": "\nimport torch\nimport torch.nn as nn\nimport torch.nn.functional as F\nfrom transformers import AutoModelForCausalLM\n\nclass ModelNew(nn.Module):\n def __init__(self, model_name, config):\n super().__init__()\n self.config = config\n self.model = AutoModelForCausalLM.from_pretrained(model_name, config=config)\n self.model.eval()\n for p in self.model.parameters():\n p.requires_grad_(False)\n\n # Precompute RoPE cos/sin for fixed seq_len=512\n head_dim = config.hidden_size // config.num_attention_heads\n inv_freq = 1.0 / (10000.0 ** (torch.arange(0, head_dim, 2, dtype=torch.float32) / head_dim))\n t = torch.arange(512, dtype=torch.float32)\n freqs = torch.outer(t, inv_freq)\n emb = torch.cat([freqs, freqs], dim=-1)\n cos = emb.cos().to(config.torch_dtype if config.torch_dtype is not None else torch.float32)\n sin = emb.sin().to(config.torch_dtype if config.torch_dtype is not None else torch.float32)\n self.register_buffer(\"cos\", cos, persistent=False)\n self.register_buffer(\"sin\", sin, persistent=False)\n\n # Precompute causal mask for fixed seq_len=512\n causal_mask = torch.triu(torch.ones(512, 512, dtype=torch.bool), diagonal=1)\n causal_mask = causal_mask.logical_not().logical_xor(causal_mask) # not needed\n # We'll create a float mask to add directly\n float_mask = torch.zeros(512, 512, dtype=torch.float32)\n float_mask = float_mask.masked_fill(causal_mask, float(\"-inf\"))\n self.register_buffer(\"causal_mask\", float_mask, persistent=False)\n\n # Extract weights layer by layer\n layers = self.model.model.layers\n self.num_layers = len(layers)\n self.hidden_size = config.hidden_size\n self.num_heads = config.num_attention_heads\n self.head_dim = self.hidden_size // self.num_heads\n self.intermediate_size = config.intermediate_size\n self.eps = config.rms_norm_eps\n\n # Store norm weights\n self.norm1_weights = nn.ParameterList()\n self.norm2_weights = nn.ParameterList()\n self.q_weights = nn.ParameterList()\n self.k_weights = nn.ParameterList()\n self.v_weights = nn.ParameterList()\n self.o_weights = nn.ParameterList()\n self.gate_weights = nn.ParameterList()\n self.up_weights = nn.ParameterList()\n self.down_weights = nn.ParameterList()\n\n for layer in layers:\n self.norm1_weights.append(layer.input_layernorm.weight)\n self.norm2_weights.append(layer.post_attention_layernorm.weight)\n self.q_weights.append(layer.self_attn.q_proj.weight)\n self.k_weights.append(layer.self_attn.k_proj.weight)\n self.v_weights.append(layer.self_attn.v_proj.weight)\n self.o_weights.append(layer.self_attn.o_proj.weight)\n self.gate_weights.append(layer.mlp.gate_proj.weight)\n self.up_weights.append(layer.mlp.up_proj.weight)\n self.down_weights.append(layer.mlp.down_proj.weight)\n\n self.final_norm_weight = self.model.model.norm.weight\n self.lm_head_weight = self.model.lm_head.weight if self.model.lm_head is not None else self.model.model.embed_tokens.weight\n self.embed_weight = self.model.model.embed_tokens.weight\n\n def rmsnorm(self, x, weight):\n x_fp32 = x.float()\n variance = x_fp32.pow(2).mean(-1, keepdim=True)\n x_norm = x_fp32 * torch.rsqrt(variance + self.eps)\n return (x_norm * weight.unsqueeze(0)).to(x.dtype)\n\n def apply_rope(self, q, k):\n # q,k: (bs, num_heads, seq_len, head_dim)\n cos = self.cos[:q.size(2)].unsqueeze(0).unsqueeze(0) # (1,1,seq,head_dim)\n sin = self.sin[:q.size(2)].unsqueeze(0).unsqueeze(0)\n q1, q2 = q[..., :self.head_dim//2], q[..., self.head_dim//2:]\n k1, k2 = k[..., :self.head_dim//2], k[..., self.head_dim//2:]\n rq = torch.cat([-q2, q1], dim=-1)\n rk = torch.cat([-k2, k1], dim=-1)\n q_embed = q * cos + rq * sin\n k_embed = k * cos + rk * sin\n return q_embed, k_embed\n\n def forward(self, input_ids):\n with torch.no_grad():\n x = F.embedding(input_ids, self.embed_weight)\n bs = x.size(0)\n seq_len = x.size(1)\n causal_mask = self.causal_mask[:seq_len, :seq_len].unsqueeze(0).unsqueeze(0)\n\n for i in range(self.num_layers):\n residual = x\n x = self.rmsnorm(x, self.norm1_weights[i])\n\n q = F.linear(x, self.q_weights[i])\n k = F.linear(x, self.k_weights[i])\n v = F.linear(x, self.v_weights[i])\n\n q = q.view(bs, seq_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2).contiguous()\n k = k.view(bs, seq_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2).contiguous()\n v = v.view(bs, seq_len, self.num_heads, self.head_dim).transpose(1, 2).contiguous()\n\n q, k = self.apply_rope(q, k)\n\n scores = torch.matmul(q, k.transpose(-2, -1))\n scores = scores / (self.head_dim ** 0.5)\n scores = scores.float() + causal_mask\n attn = torch.softmax(scores, dim=-1).to(q.dtype)\n out = torch.matmul(attn, v)\n out = out.transpose(1, 2).reshape(bs, seq_len, self.hidden_size)\n\n x = residual + F.linear(out, self.o_weights[i])\n\n residual = x\n x = self.rmsnorm(x, self.norm2_weights[i])\n\n gate = F.linear(x, self.gate_weights[i])\n up = F.linear(x, self.up_weights[i])\n activated = F.silu(gate) * up\n x = residual + F.linear(activated, self.down_weights[i])\n\n x = self.rmsnorm(x, self.final_norm_weight)\n logits = F.linear(x, self.lm_head_weight)\n return logits\n" }
